    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8952226102730845413">The first science fiction written by a black woman, <i>Kindred</i> has become a cornerstone of black American literature. This combination of slave memoir, fantasy, and historical fiction is a novel of rich literary complexity. Having just celebrated her 26th birthday in 1976 California, Dana, an African-American woman, is suddenly and inexplicably wrenched through time into ante</span>
  <span id="freeText8952226102730845413" style="display:none">The first science fiction written by a black woman, <i>Kindred</i> has become a cornerstone of black American literature. This combination of slave memoir, fantasy, and historical fiction is a novel of rich literary complexity. Having just celebrated her 26th birthday in 1976 California, Dana, an African-American woman, is suddenly and inexplicably wrenched through time into antebellum Maryland. After saving a drowning white boy there, she finds herself staring into the barrel of a shotgun and is transported back to the present just in time to save her life. During numerous such time-defying episodes with the same young man, she realizes the challenge she’s been given...</span>
  <a data-text-id="8952226102730845413" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

Quite early in the book you know what she is doing and why. <span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This book is definitely for an older reader - the book includes rape, beatings, and mutilations. Racially charged language including frequent use of t<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This book is definitely for an older reader - the book includes rape, beatings, and mutilations. Racially charged language including frequent use of the "N-word" may be problematic if your family has not discussed race in depth before. I would suggest at least 16 if a solo read, maybe 14-15 if you have a mature child and plan to discuss it with them as they go.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1693814810" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1693814810" />
    <a title="Rick Riordan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25375513-rick-riordan"><img alt="Rick Riordan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442449448p2/25375513.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1693814810?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rick Riordan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rick Riordan" href="/user/show/25375513-rick-riordan">Rick Riordan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1693814810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18370438625508827629">After reading Parable of the Sower, I had to go right out and buy Butler’s most famous novel Kindred. I was not disappointed. It is amazing that this book was written in 1976 and feels just as fresh and timely in 2016. Dana, a young African American woman who has just started a career as a writer in California, is suddenly and inexplicably yanked back in time to Maryland in 1815, where she must save a white boy named Rufus from drowning.<br/><br/>This becomes only the first of many time traveling episodes</span>
  <span id="freeText18370438625508827629" style="display:none">After reading Parable of the Sower, I had to go right out and buy Butler’s most famous novel Kindred. I was not disappointed. It is amazing that this book was written in 1976 and feels just as fresh and timely in 2016. Dana, a young African American woman who has just started a career as a writer in California, is suddenly and inexplicably yanked back in time to Maryland in 1815, where she must save a white boy named Rufus from drowning.<br /><br />This becomes only the first of many time traveling episodes for Dana. She quickly realizes that Rufus is one of her own ancestors, mentioned in the family Bible. Somehow, they are connected across time because they are kindred. To assure her own future, Dana must keep Rufus alive until he has children who will some day be Dana’s family line. Unfortunately, Rufus gets in a lot of trouble.<br /><br />Only moments pass in the modern world each time Dana is called away, but months or even years pass in the world of 1815. Dana watches Rufus grow from a little boy into an adult slave owner who inherits his father’s plantation. She tries her best to influence Rufus’ development, but can she overcome the poisonous institution of slavery that infects everyone it touches?<br /><br />The novel is a potent metaphor for the modern African American experience and the American experience in general. We may be lulled into the feeling that we have advanced, that we have made progress as a society. But at any moment, we may be yanked back into the past and reminded of where we came from. That heritage of slavery, exploitation and racism is an integral part of our national identity, and it is never far below the surface. It can overcome us in an instant. Like Dana, we must be constantly on guard, well-equipped and ready to be yanked out of our supposedly modern and enlightened existence to deal with the ugliest parts of our nature. We are kindred with the Americans of 1815, whether we like it or not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18370438625508827629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1573948233?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2016">2016</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1573948233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10675047946304729652"><blockquote>
  <i>“The ease. Us, the children… I never realized how easily people could be trained to accept slavery.”</i>
</blockquote><br/>Butler is an author that constantly pops up on "Best sci-fi" and "Must-Read African American authors" lists and I can finally see why. This book may be my first by her, but it won't be my last. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60931.Kindred' title='Kindred by Octavia E. Butler' rel='nofollow noopener'>Kindred</a> is a <b>fascinating, horrific journey through a dark time in American history</b>, combining eye-opening historical research with time travel.<br/><br/>I suppose some modern readers will want to compare this sto</span>
  <span id="freeText10675047946304729652" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“The ease. Us, the children… I never realized how easily people could be trained to accept slavery.”</i>
</blockquote><br />Butler is an author that constantly pops up on "Best sci-fi" and "Must-Read African American authors" lists and I can finally see why. This book may be my first by her, but it won't be my last. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60931.Kindred" title="Kindred by Octavia E. Butler" rel="nofollow noopener">Kindred</a> is a <b>fascinating, horrific journey through a dark time in American history</b>, combining eye-opening historical research with time travel.<br /><br />I suppose some modern readers will want to compare this story to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10964.Outlander__Outlander___1_" title="Outlander (Outlander, #1) by Diana Gabaldon" rel="nofollow noopener">Outlander</a> and there are some similarities - a woman trying to survive in the past, lots of blood-soaked history and horror, the harsh realities of being who you are in that time - but not only did this book come first, but it is far more distressing, more tied in with historical truth, and way more about surviving than it is about lusty scenes with a kilted hot dude.<br /><br /><b>It's a really important "what if" book about race</b>. What if a modern black woman suddenly found herself transported 150+ years into the past, right into the centre of the antebellum South? The book doesn't shy away from portraying the realities of that (nothing is sugar-coated, be prepared for some upsetting scenes). <br /><br />But it's also more than a gruesome look at historical racism and violence. There are many complex and interesting characters - both slaves and slave owners. Butler has written a book that goes deeper than surface level, exploring how people come to accept slavery as the norm and to justify poor treatment of slaves. Dana is horrified how easy it is. And so was I.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60931.Kindred" title="Kindred by Octavia E. Butler" rel="nofollow noopener">Kindred</a> is so good because, not only is it well-written and emotionally effective, but it also manages to be several different important things: complex historical-fiction, intriguing science-fiction, and a memoir of slavery. For a novel so obviously fictional, it feels very real and true. Maybe because, sadly, most of it is. <br /><br />I know this is one book that will stay with me for a long time.<br /><br /><a href="http://thebookgeek.org" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.facebook.com/thebookgeekreviews" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/thebookgeek01" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://instagram.com/emleemay" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://www.youtube.com/c/EmilyMayTheBookGeek" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a> | <a href="https://society6.com/bookgeek/" rel="nofollow noopener">Store</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10675047946304729652" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1350996347" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1350996347" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1350996347?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1350996347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8507968814024373707">Octavia Butler is an amazing writer. If you enjoy reading SF/F, or even an interest in speculative fiction, you would like her work.<br/><br/>Kindred, first published in 1979, would become her most best-selling novel.<br/><br/>This is also a painful book to read because of its graphic depiction of slavery and Butler wastes no time in demonizing what was demonic. Describing the slave life from the perspective of a time-travelling modern woman, Butler’s strong narrative prose is in high form for a low burden – to ill</span>
  <span id="freeText8507968814024373707" style="display:none">Octavia Butler is an amazing writer. If you enjoy reading SF/F, or even an interest in speculative fiction, you would like her work.<br><br>Kindred, first published in 1979, would become her most best-selling novel.<br><br>This is also a painful book to read because of its graphic depiction of slavery and Butler wastes no time in demonizing what was demonic. Describing the slave life from the perspective of a time-travelling modern woman, Butler’s strong narrative prose is in high form for a low burden – to illustrate to contemporary readers the horrors of slavery and in this context to draw a comparison with life of our time, making the transition to the early 1800s all the more stark and evil in contrast.<br><br>Kindred is also an allegory for our modern times, still burdened by the wounds of slavery and a racial consciousness in our society that has scars that won’t heal. Butler shows us, though, that we as a nation and a people are bound, as kindred, between races and with a shared history.<br><br>Back in the 90s I was working in Washington DC and I had the opportunity to meet a group of folks from Africa. Multi-lingual (with French predominant among the diplomats) I found the people I met worldly, intelligent, generous and interesting to talk to – and they were singularly not American. I think this was the first time I had met a large group of people from another continent and the idea struck me how much closer I was to my black neighbors than I was to these people I had just met. <br><br>Butler adeptly reveals in Kindred, in multiple ways, the many degrees of our shared humanity. But more narrowly, Butler is pointing out our kinship as Americans, dates like 1976 and July 4th must be intentional, how the shared history of slavery – between black Americans and white Americans – has bound us together.<br><br>Butler also, once again, has created a strong female protagonist in Dana whose endurance and courage are remarkable, made more evident by the fact that she has a unique viewpoint. Dana, in some respects, becomes a symbol of a present-day African-American woman, both made stronger from her heritage, but also still bearing the wounds of past wrongs.<br><br>Kindred also displays Butler’s amazing talents in storytelling, using dramatic irony expertly. A reader may notice a subtle, though strangely twisted reference to a scene in Lee’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2657.To_Kill_a_Mockingbird" title="To Kill a Mockingbird by Harper Lee" rel="nofollow noopener">To Kill a Mockingbird</a>.<br><br>Introspective and somber, with many questions that remain unanswered, Kindred is a powerful work told by an artist of genius ability.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1556909798i/27451404._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="230" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="8507968814024373707" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1134791936" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1134791936" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3404638545353667991">DNF at 50% (with some skipping) <br/><br/>What came first, the egg or the chicken? What came first, the badly written book or the reading slump? Hard questions to answer but one thing is certain. It definitely did not help forcing me to reach 50% of this book. I only did it because of my rating rule and because I wanted to bitch about it. So here it is again the time for an unpopular opinion. <br/><br/>I though this book to be TERRIBLE. I don’t even know with what to start. I understand it is written by a woman of </span>
  <span id="freeText3404638545353667991" style="display:none">DNF at 50% (with some skipping) <br /><br />What came first, the egg or the chicken? What came first, the badly written book or the reading slump? Hard questions to answer but one thing is certain. It definitely did not help forcing me to reach 50% of this book. I only did it because of my rating rule and because I wanted to bitch about it. So here it is again the time for an unpopular opinion. <br /><br />I though this book to be TERRIBLE. I don’t even know with what to start. I understand it is written by a woman of color in a time when it was an extraordinary accomplishment. I get and admire that. I also get that she had an agenda to prove how easily one can accept slavery, even in our modern world. However, the above is not a relevant excuse for bad writing, cartoonish characters, poorly conceived plot and ridiculous dialogue. Also, the use of time travel had nothing to do with SF, there was no explanation of the phenomenon, and it felt only as a lazy gimmick to prove her point. Yes, others used it as well but better, in my opinion. <br /><br />Let’s start with the plot. We are in 1976 America, a young black woman is married with a white man and she suddenly starts to repeatedly go back in time in the antebellum South so she can save a child (and later young man) who proves to be her ancestor. It quickly becomes obvious that she has to save him every time he is in trouble, otherwise she would not exist in the present time. So far so good, the premise sounds interesting. <br /><br />Too bad the execution was poor. Firstly, the two pair accepted way to easy the time travelling part. The same happened with the people in the past. You tell me that a person in the 19th century would not freak out and try to murder any source of such an abomination? The dialogues between the husband and wife after the first two times she comes back are laughable. At first he doesn’t believe her although she disappeared and then he doesn’t understand why she is scared. Really? I would lose control of myself it that happened, screaming my ass into a mental hospital. <br /><br />Later, when they both land in the past, I could not believe how easily they get used to the roles they had to play there, her as a slave and him as the white master. I totally understand that she had to lay low in order to survive but that doesn’t mean she had to be acceptant in her mind or find excuses for that little piece of shit, Rufus. And to convinced Alice that is ok to be raped so you can survive…. Nope.<br /><br />I disliked all the characters, especially Dana and her detachment; the author did not make me feel anything except annoyance. I know I am in minority here but it can’t be helped, I can’t find many positive thoughts about this novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3404638545353667991" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_967107490" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer967107490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1826087329625204272">Octavia Butler amazes me. She writes science fiction that is full of complicated ideas about race and sexuality that are completely readable. I’ll innocently start reading, thinking only to get a solid start on the book, and suddenly discover I’m halfway through the story. That isn’t to imply she’s a light-weight, however; her works are emotionally and ethically dense, the subject of numerous high school and college essays. A recent read of Dawn inspired a number of recommendations for Butler an</span>
  <span id="freeText1826087329625204272" style="display:none">Octavia Butler amazes me. She writes science fiction that is full of complicated ideas about race and sexuality that are completely readable. I’ll innocently start reading, thinking only to get a solid start on the book, and suddenly discover I’m halfway through the story. That isn’t to imply she’s a light-weight, however; her works are emotionally and ethically dense, the subject of numerous high school and college essays. A recent read of Dawn inspired a number of recommendations for Butler and a buddy read of her book Kindred. <br /><br />*************<br /><br />Full reviews at my blog: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2014/06/17/kindred-by-octavia-butler/">http://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2014/0...</a><br /><br /><br />Why? Because 1) gramazon can't have my reviews, 2) because I don't feel like being censored according to some twit's whims.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1826087329625204272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27985975?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M—" class="user" itemprop="url" name="M—" href="/user/show/1353617-m">M—</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780807083055" href="/book/show/616830.Kindred">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1353617-m?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27985975" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17879102447198995581">On October 5, 2004, Octavia E. Butler visited my graduate university to give a lecture and book signing. I was really impressed by her. She actually spent several hours at the university, giving a public interview with one of the professors, then a short lecture to a large auditorium, then a book signing. I even skipped class in order to attend. <br/><br/>The interview was really fascinating, where Butler answered questions about how she worked to write <i>Kindred</i> and how she felt about the characters and ho</span>
  <span id="freeText17879102447198995581" style="display:none">On October 5, 2004, Octavia E. Butler visited my graduate university to give a lecture and book signing. I was really impressed by her. She actually spent several hours at the university, giving a public interview with one of the professors, then a short lecture to a large auditorium, then a book signing. I even skipped class in order to attend. <br /><br />The interview was really fascinating, where Butler answered questions about how she worked to write <i>Kindred</i> and how she felt about the characters and how the result all turned out. The professor kind of threw Butler for a loop once, when she pulled an interpretation of the book out of left field, and Butler blinked, and slowly said she didn't write with that interpretation at all in mind, but that she was of the opinion that any interpretation the reader reaches is a valid one. I thought she handled the question particularly well. <br /><br />In the lecture, Butler talked mostly about how she writes, her writing style, her relationship with her fans, and the book she was currently writing, <i>Fledgling.</i> The signing afterwards was very informal, but I didn’t try to stay and chat. Butler had lots of professors and awestruck students who were all trying to catch her attention. I got my book signed, said a polite thank you, and left happy. <br /><br /><i>Fledgling</i> turned out to be the last book Butler wrote. She died unexpectedly in early 2006. I feel incredibly fortunate to have had the chance to meet her. <br /><br />The book: Was good. A time-traveling story dealing with love, gender, race, racism, and responsibility. It was beautifully and rather painfully done. I never would have found it if it hadn’t been for the author visit, and I’m rather sad about that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17879102447198995581" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1689646321" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1689646321" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1689646321?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781472214812" href="/book/show/21020588-kindred">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Cecily by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=scifi-future-speculative-fict">scifi-future-speculative-fict</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=historical-fict-pre-20th-c">historical-fict-pre-20th-c</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=read-only-cos-of-gr-friends">read-only-cos-of-gr-friends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=usa-and-canada">usa-and-canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1689646321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2395552421408619555"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1468694555i/19737880._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>“<i>I never realized how easily people could be trained to accept slavery.</i>” <br/><br/>I wanted to love this book. I knew the slave narrative might be harrowing (though it’s not overly graphic), but it has an average GR rating over four stars, features time-travel dilemmas, has a strong, intelligent, kind, and practical female protagonist, and gives thought-provoking insights into the complexity of US race relations in the 1800s and, to a lesser extent, the 1970s. <br/><br/>It is a good and powerful, exciting and educ</span>
  <span id="freeText2395552421408619555" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1468694555i/19737880._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>“<i>I never realized how easily people could be trained to accept slavery.</i>” <br><br>I wanted to love this book. I knew the slave narrative might be harrowing (though it’s not overly graphic), but it has an average GR rating over four stars, features time-travel dilemmas, has a strong, intelligent, kind, and practical female protagonist, and gives thought-provoking insights into the complexity of US race relations in the 1800s and, to a lesser extent, the 1970s. <br><br>It is a good and powerful, exciting and educational book. But something didn’t quite connect for me. I hoped that composing my thoughts would make me see it in a more favourable light - and it has. Perhaps I just read it too fast to digest it properly.<br><br><b>Kindred</b> <br><br>One word, seven letters, but several interpretations, all with emotional impact. <br><br>* The most common and literal meaning relates to ties of blood: our immediate family. The kindred we can’t choose, even if we hate or despise them.<br><br>* But blood is too narrow to include one’s partner, or any adopted children, honorary uncles and aunts, or step parents. And what about children born to slave women who could never claim their father/owner’s family as kindred, even if they wanted to?<br><br>* So it widens to “kindred spirits” - our closest friends and allies, with whom we share attitudes, experiences, and interests. Regardless of biological parentage, a slave child’s kindred can only be fellow slaves.<br><br>* Ultimately, Butler’s message is that black and white (and brown and pink and yellow), male and female (and everything else), <b>we are all kindred. One race: the human race</b>. Race as a social construct. (See <a href="http://www.livescience.com/53613-race-is-social-construct-not-scientific.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Live Science</a> and <a href="http://bigthink.com/think-tank/bill-nye-race-is-a-social-construct" rel="nofollow noopener">Bill Nye</a>.)<br><br>This is not a Christian book, and I am not a Christian person, but I was reminded of the message of Jesus’ parable of The Good Samaritan. The man who asked “Who is my neighbour?” was shown that the answer was everyone in need. That’s a tough message to apply, but given the turmoil in the word right now, it is as important as it ever was. <br><br><b>Plot - No Spoilers</b><br><br>The book is easy to summarise in a way that gives no more spoilers than the first three pages and back cover. Dana is a 26-year old middle class African-American living in 1976 LA with her white husband, Kevin. Somehow, she comes unstuck in time (like Billy Pilgrim in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129649718" rel="nofollow noopener">Slaughterhouse Five</a> and Henry in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23327825" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Traveller’s Wife</a>) and suddenly finds herself on a plantation in Maryland in 1815. <br><br>This happens several times, over twenty years of 19th century time, with the usual issues and dilemmas of time travel, but that is just the mechanism for depicting the horrors of slavery, and the complex power and sexual relationships that result, as well as exploring the source of hatred (nature versus nurture), acquiescence, revenge, and the types and possibility of redemption and freedom. <br><br>Kindred is more historical and political adventure than sci-fi. It’s fast-paced and, despite the subject, quite an easy read. And the ending is satisfying, but not ludicrously sentimental or tidy.<br><br><b>Owning and Being Owned</b><br><br>In 1976, Dana is proud of her independence, having repeatedly fought to do what she wanted, rather than settle for what was expected. In the 19th century, she has to consider the terrifying risks and consequences of striving for even a tiny bit of independence. The power-play between master and slave can acquire aspects of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stockholm_syndrome" rel="nofollow noopener">Stockholm Syndrome</a>.<br><br>Although the story is told by Dana and she is the central character, at least as important is Rufus Weyland, son of the plantation owner. The way his attitude and behaviour change as he grows up is echoed in the more recent <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/186159184" rel="nofollow noopener">The Help</a>, though it is more complex here. As a small child, he’s allowed to have slave children as friends, even as his father buys and sells their families, beats and sleeps with them. <br><br>Gradually, Rufus develops an unrequited “destructive single-minded love” for two women that I never fully understood. With one woman, it’s sexual, so he repeatedly rapes her. <br><br>“<i>There was no shame in raping a black woman, but there could be shame in loving one</i>.”<br><br>Of course, Rufus doesn’t think of it as rape because she doesn’t try to stop him and, more importantly, he owns her. So the woman endures, but “She forgave him nothing, forgot nothing.”<br><br>Dana sees how manipulative Rufus is, she experiences it herself. She sees the bad in him and occasionally slivers of good. She tries to enlighten him, but is remarkably forgiving when he follows in his father’s footsteps. More so than I could be, which is perhaps another reason why it didn’t quite ring true for me. <br><br><b>He’s of his Time. Does that make it OK?</b><br><br>“<i>He wasn’t a monster at all. Just an ordinary man who sometimes did the monstrous things his society said were legal and proper.</i>”<br><br>A common debate on GR and in the wider world (yes, there is one, I’m led to believe), is to what extent we judge those in the past by the standards of our time. <br><br>* Should we cast aside books by people who we now know had hateful views and who maybe did hateful deeds? <br><br>* Should books about the past be sanitised and redacted to make them acceptable to modern sensibilities?<br><br>Dana is confronted by this dilemma in a more direct and personal way. She wants to teach Rufus to think of and treat his slaves kindly, but as his views become darker and more complicated, her opinion of him is ever more conflicted - exacerbated by the power he has over her. <br><br>* How much of what happens can be blamed on the surrounding cultural norms? <br><br>* Does a slave owner who beats relatively rarely and gently deserve leniency?<br><br><b>Identity: Colour, Gender, Social Rank, Ancestry</b><br><br>Who is Dana, and how free is she? In both periods she can be seen as, and is sometimes called, “a white nigger”. In 1815, she is assumed to be a slave just because of her colour, all the more inferior because she's female. But the fact she talks white and educated causes confusion, resentment, and conflict. And she comes to realise that even in 1976, she is not entirely free of her heritage, despite her relative detachment from it (though she has read at least some of her ten books about black history even before she has a specific need to do so).<br><br>There are similar questions for many other characters, especially slaves who consider running away in the hope of freedom (or death), those who stay because they want to keep their children, and those who trade privilege and suffering (such as sleeping with a boss they hate to have slightly gentler conditions).<br><br>I could write a whole review about her husband, Kevin: how he - and their marriage - is changed by her experiences, and his. But I won't this time; it's interesting and important, but secondary. <br><br>The other huge aspect is ancestry, and how that defines one's identity, both in terms of racial identity, but also in terms of character. What if you are appalled by who and what your forebears do are and do? (An issue those who research their family trees often have to face.)<br><br><b>Words and Language</b><br><br>This is a book you read for the ideas and story, rather than the language. But Butler makes her words work in a book that’s barely 300 pages: a single word title, and short, elemental chapter titles: The River, The Fire, The Fall, The Fight, The Storm, The Rope. <br><br>Of course, “the N word” is used frequently. Given the setting, it would be bizarre if it were not.<br><br><b>Human Pantone</b><br><br>The image at the top is from this short blog post about race:<br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.laurassoapbox.net/2012/08/puppies-and-kangaroos.html">http://www.laurassoapbox.net/2012/08/...</a><br>But it is actually part of Angélica Daas’ “Human Pantone” art project, which I saw on posters in Bilbao earlier this year:<br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://brazigzag.com/culture/angelica-dass-humanae-pantone-series/">http://brazigzag.com/culture/angelica...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2395552421408619555" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_305618564" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/305618564" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/305618564?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sf-top-20">sf-top-20</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=top-20">top-20</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer305618564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2791342060616774707">I had no idea what Kindred is about prior to reading it, I previously read Octavia Butler's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52318.Wild_Seed__Patternmaster___1_' title='Wild Seed (Patternmaster, #1) by Octavia E. Butler' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wild Seed</a> and thought it was marvelous, and Kindred seems to be her most popular work judging by Goodreads ratings. So buying a copy of Kindred without knowing anything about it was a no-brainer. I even deliberately avoided looking at the book's synopsis before hand, I just wanted to get to know the book as I read on. I hoped for a pleasant surprise, which I did get. This is only the second Octavia Butler</span>
  <span id="freeText2791342060616774707" style="display:none">I had no idea what Kindred is about prior to reading it, I previously read Octavia Butler's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52318.Wild_Seed__Patternmaster___1_" title="Wild Seed (Patternmaster, #1) by Octavia E. Butler" rel="nofollow noopener">Wild Seed</a> and thought it was marvelous, and Kindred seems to be her most popular work judging by Goodreads ratings. So buying a copy of Kindred without knowing anything about it was a no-brainer. I even deliberately avoided looking at the book's synopsis before hand, I just wanted to get to know the book as I read on. I hoped for a pleasant surprise, which I did get. This is only the second Octavia Butler book I have read and I already worship her.<br><br>Kindred is about Dana, an African American woman who finds herself time travelling involuntarily to Maryland in the early nineteenth century. It is not explained how or why this happen to her, the mechanic of it is entirely irrelevant to the story. The novel is about her experience of slavery in the past. Her fate becomes intertwined with Rufus, a white ancestor who is the only son of a plantation owner and who somehow triggers her time traveling trips every time he is in mortal danger, a situation that arises more frequently to him than to most people. While there she experiences the woes of slavery first hand, including whipping, beating, degradation and humiliation.<br><br>This is a harrowing and emotional read, I almost cry manly tears during some of the chapters. I never pondered what it may have been like to be a slave, it is not exactly a contingency which is at all likely to ever arise. However, Ms Butler - genius that she was - made me <i>feel</i> it through the eyes of her protagonist. The pains and humiliation of slavery resonates with me even though there ought to be nothing to resonate. I kind of winced every time a stroke of a whip is described. This is not a comfortable read but highly engrossing and thought provoking. The book is very much character-centric, the relationship between Dana and Rufus is very complex and fascinating. Dana's husband Kevin who also become embroiled in time traveling and is marooned in the nineteenth century for years without his wife adds to her complications, his reaction to returning to the present time (1976) is entirely believable and again resonates strongly.<br><br>The book reminds me a little of Connie Willis's excellent <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983.Doomsday_Book__Oxford_Time_Travel___1_" title="Doomsday Book (Oxford Time Travel, #1) by Connie Willis" rel="nofollow noopener">Doomsday Book</a>, which is about time travelling to the fourteenth century and also a harrowing (yet wonderful) read, though the emphasis of that book is on poverty, hardship and diseases rather than slavery. The involuntary time traveling aspect of the book reminds me of Audrey Niffenegger's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14050.The_Time_Traveler_s_Wife" title="The Time Traveler's Wife by Audrey Niffenegger" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Traveler's Wife</a>, though Kindred predates it, and Kindred is certainly not a romantic book. <br><br>Octavia Butler was not one of those literary writers who try to avoid the science fiction label like the plague even while using sf tropes in their works, she has always loved sf and gladly embraced the genre (see photo below).<br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/r5cag" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1379775603i/344824._SX540_.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>That said, Kindred is also not science fiction. The author described it as a "grim fantasy" and deliberately did not put any science in it, it is described by some literary critics as a "neo-slave narrative". I did consider why the book was written as a fantasy (or almost sf) instead of historical fiction, then I realised that it was probably done so the modern reader can experience the nineteenth century Maryland through the protagonist's contemporary eyes, this makes the book very visceral.<br><br>While the book was written to make the reader ponder some serious issues such as man's inhumanity to man, inequality and courage in an environment where you are made to feel worthless, at no point did I feel like being lectured to. The author knows the importance of communicating through the story, and I was completely swept away by it. Whatever I read next will likely suffer from being compared to this book. This goes in my all-time greats list.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1502851922i/23602938._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="127" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><u>Notes</u>:<br>• From Tor.com: <a href="http://www.tor.com/2015/06/22/on-this-day-octavia-butler/" rel="nofollow noopener">Octavia Butler Will Change the Way You Look at Genre Fiction</a>.<br>• <a href="http://www.tor.com/2016/01/29/octavia-butler-note-of-encouragement/" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a> is another reason to love Octavia Butler.<br><br>• Interesting background info from <a href="https://theportalist.com/15-fascinating-facts-about-octavia-butler" rel="nofollow noopener">The Portalist</a>:<br><br>Kindred was inspired by the time a very young Butler spent with her mother at work. Butler told In Motion Magazine in 2004 that a lot of the motivation behind her novel Kindred "came when I was in preschool, when my mother used to take me to work with her." <br><br>Kindred follows Dana, a writer who travels back in time to the antebellum South and meets her ancestors, a white plantation owner and a Black slave. The novel argues for the courageousness of people existing under unimaginable circumstances, as Dana makes compromises in order to survive slavery. Butler's own mother was a housemaid, and many of Butler's earliest memories were of the degradations her mother endured at work. She told In Motion that witnessing the racism her mother put up with in order to bring Butler a better life helped inspire much of Kindred's message: <br><br>"I got to see her not hearing insults and going in back doors, and even though I was a little kid, I realized it was humiliating. I knew something was wrong, it was unpleasant, it was bad. I remember saying to her a little later, at seven or eight, "I'll never do what you do, what you do is terrible." And she just got this sad look on her face and didn't say anything. I think it was the look and the memory of the indignities she endured. I just remembered that and wanted to convey that people who underwent all this were not cowards, were not people who were just too pathetic to protect themselves, but were heroes because they were using what they had to help their kids get a little further."<br><br><a href="https://www.coursehero.com/lit/Kindred/infographic/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1502853751i/23603046._SY540_.jpg" width="89" height="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br>Excellent Kindred Infographic (with spoilers) click on image to see full size.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2791342060616774707" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1842921279" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1842921279" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567953607p2/2018505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1842921279?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Thomas by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/29990253-ninoshkka">Ninoshkka</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=2nd-favorites">2nd-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=five-stars">five-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1842921279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6543762807372160084">I wish we could read more authors like Octavia Butler, bell hooks, and Celeste Ng in our English classes instead of white men like Ernest Hemingway. I loved <i>Kindred</i> because it uses the science-fiction/fantasy genre to expose the cruelties and horrors of slavery and racism in an innovative way. Similar to what author Viet Thanh Nguyen writes in his book <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1615797879?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Nothing Ever Dies</i>
</a>, the United States's education system often informs us of issues like war and slavery through a sanitized, depoliticized le</span>
  <span id="freeText6543762807372160084" style="display:none">I wish we could read more authors like Octavia Butler, bell hooks, and Celeste Ng in our English classes instead of white men like Ernest Hemingway. I loved <i>Kindred</i> because it uses the science-fiction/fantasy genre to expose the cruelties and horrors of slavery and racism in an innovative way. Similar to what author Viet Thanh Nguyen writes in his book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1615797879?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Nothing Ever Dies</i>
</a>, the United States's education system often informs us of issues like war and slavery through a sanitized, depoliticized lens. Though we "learn" about these events, we do not recognize the cruelty and evil our country's past generations committed - and how we are also complicit if we do not act for justice today. Octavia Butler's <i>Kindred</i> tells a gripping tale and reminds us of how we must not let the stories of our past happen again.<br /><br /><i>Kindred</i> follows 26-year-old Dana, a black woman who lives in California and gets transported to the antebellum South. There, she meets Rufus, the white son of a plantation owner who will go on to sire the daughter who becomes Dana's ancestor. Dana is teleported back in time over and over again to protect Rufus from death, but each time she travels to the past, she encounters increasing amounts of danger and abuse that put her own life at risk for extinction.<br /><br />Butler creates a compelling cast of characters in <i>Kindred</i>. Dana, Kevin, Rufus, Alice, etc. all have complex motivations and their relationships with one another feel replete with nuanced power dynamics, as well as love and hate and fear. In addition to imbuing <i>Kindred</i> with a fast-moving and surprising plot, Butler succeeds at showing and not telling the atrocities of slavery through Dana's travels backward and forward in time, in particular her forced journey to acclimate to plantation life in the nineteenth century. Through detailing the pain Dana suffers and the pain she sees her fellow enslaved individuals suffer at the hands of white folk, Butler encourages us to consider the challenges of surviving in an unjust world, just like the one we live in now. Why is it that our ancestors, as well as a lot of us today, are so willing to look away from the evils of racism? How do we stay true to our values in a society that so often pits minorities against each other, gives power to those who disempowers others, etc.? <i>Kindred</i> makes us think about these questions without offering simple answers, providing proof of its thoughtfulness and strength as a novel.<br /><br />Overall, a book I would recommend to anyone and everyone. I honestly feel ashamed at my younger self for not reading authors like Butler sooner and for buying into problematic portrayals of slavery, like Margaret Mitchell's <i>Gone With the Wind</i>. I am doing my best to make up for it now by reading more books about social justice, by donating to the ACLU and the Southern Poverty Law Center and other organizations fighting the good fight, and by having conversations about these topics, volunteering, etc. As a companion to <i>Kindred</i>, I would recommend reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1335106425?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The New Jim Crow</i>
</a> by Michelle Alexander and giving to groups that fight mass incarceration, as that injustice serves in many ways as the slavery of our time. Thank you to Ms. Butler for creating art that allows us to see injustice and to fight it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6543762807372160084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1923584014" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1923584014" />
    <a title="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31461418-alienor-french-frowner"><img alt="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532688964p2/31461418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1923584014?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" href="/user/show/31461418-alienor-french-frowner">Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=adult-fiction">adult-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=trigger-warning">trigger-warning</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=diverse-reads-2017">diverse-reads-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=2017-reads">2017-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1923584014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3676943478904896939"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1488197359i/22088423._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I remember the astonished fear I felt when I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6181.If_This_Is_a_Man_The_Truce?ac=1&from_search=true' rel='nofollow noopener'>Primo Levi</a> in High-school and realized <strong>how easily one can go along with dehumanization in order to save his life.</strong> As much as we humans like hiding behind false truths, we're merely trying to go easy on ourselves and to maintain our breakable feeling of control. We don't control shit. From the moment I read Holocaust accounts, I've met a lot of people assuring me that these days wouldn't ever happen again because people would fight harder and long</span>
  <span id="freeText3676943478904896939" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1488197359i/22088423._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I remember the astonished fear I felt when I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6181.If_This_Is_a_Man_The_Truce?ac=1&amp;from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">Primo Levi</a> in High-school and realized <strong>how easily one can go along with dehumanization in order to save his life.</strong> As much as we humans like hiding behind false truths, we're merely trying to go easy on ourselves and to maintain our breakable feeling of control. We don't control shit. From the moment I read Holocaust accounts, I've met a lot of people assuring me that these days wouldn't ever happen again because people would fight harder and longer. Ha. This fallacious argument first forgets that <em>it already happened again</em>, and secondly <strong>it dismisses way too quickly how readily people accept awful behaviors if they become the norm</strong>. We can hate ourselves for that, but I'm not sure what we're trying to achieve when we forget that. There will always be people who fight, but they'll often be fewer than those who silently accept or participates in the dehumanization. Now how can we change that is the real question.<br><blockquote>
  
    <strong>“The ease. Us, the children... I never realized how easily people could be trained to accept slavery.”</strong>
  
</blockquote><br>This is what makes <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60931.Kindred" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <strong>Kindred</strong>
</a> <strong>both so interesting and horrifying</strong> : Dana, a 25 years old black woman, is transported from 1976 to 1815 whenever her ancestor - Rufus - needs her to stay alive, straight into the plantation his father runs. What follows is <strong>an unflinching and very important look at what slavery really was and how its mechanisms worked</strong>, without, for once (thank you thank you), an ounce of romanticization, but rather a complex but unforgiving portrayal of what many books would sell out as a Good Master (ugh). <strong>This is what the world needs</strong>. I often mention my students in my reviews, but honestly, it's because I often feel that we ask more of children than we do of fucking adults. At ten, they're able to understand that <strong>their intend doesn't mean anything if they hurt someone : they still have to be held accountable</strong>. The world needs to hear that as white people, we might not intend to comfort and sustain white supremacy, yet every time we buy into some romanticized version of slavery, we <strong>do</strong>. A slaver who falls in love with one of his slaves is still very much a monster in my book. So, what? The guy has <em>feelings?</em> SO WHAT? <strong>He'll still buy and sell people as if they were furniture. He'll still make them work, hurt them, for his sole gain.</strong> What <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60931.Kindred" rel="nofollow noopener">Kindred</a> shows the reader is that no matter how easily we could feel sorry for said slaver - as Dana sometimes does - <strong>it doesn't change a thing</strong>. It should never change a thing.<br><blockquote>
  
    <strong>“Rufus had caused her trouble, and now he had been rewarded for it. It made no sense. No matter how kindly he treated her now that he had destroyed her, it made no sense.” </strong>
  
</blockquote><br>Served with <strong>a compelling and frightening plot</strong>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60931.Kindred" rel="nofollow noopener">Kindred</a> won't let you look away and will capture your all being until the very last page - if that's not the mark of great books, what is? This novel is absolutely terrifying and it doesn't need any zombies to be : people are the monsters. White people are, and the fact that it actually happened in history makes it even more <strong>chilling</strong>. More, if we look at History as a whole, slavery has stopped in the US such a short time ago. And if <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60931.Kindred" rel="nofollow noopener">Kindred</a> reminds us of something that we should have never forgotten, it's <strong>how easily we come to adapt to - or make the best of, how horrible that can sound - such an horrendous system.</strong> In the end, we humans want to live. This ongoing thirst might make us able to do great things, but it also makes it harder for us to fight.<br><blockquote>
  
    <strong>“Repressive societies always seemed to understand the danger of “wrong” ideas.”</strong>
  
</blockquote><br>I wish this novel would be more famous worldwide, because if it's apparently studied in High-school in the US, I had never heard of it before last year - as I found, it was translated into French in the 2000s but by such an unknown publisher, it's a shame. <strong>When are we starting to translate - and promote - these important books rather than the last NA by Colleen Hoover?</strong> Really? As the first science-fiction novel published by a black woman, and as a fucking amazing book that will linger in my mind for so long, because I'm neither able to forget these complex and fascinating characters nor the message they carry, <strong>I'd say the world should wake the fuck up and read this book. Now.</strong><br><br><strong>TW -</strong> Slavery, Rape &amp; Attempted Rape, Graphic Violence, Use of several ableist slurs (crazy, retarded)<br><br><i>For more of my reviews, please visit:</i><br><blockquote>
  <a href="https://meetthebookworld.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487851828i/22057164._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="3676943478904896939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1917116561" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1917116561" />
    <a title="Christy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11284813-christy"><img alt="Christy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594293998p2/11284813.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1917116561?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christy" href="/user/show/11284813-christy">Christy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11284813-christy?shelf=2017-audio">2017-audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11284813-christy?shelf=2017-four-stars">2017-four-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1917116561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7952298507458708192"><i>Kindred</i> is about a woman named Dana who gets transported/time-travels back to the past. She travels way back to the time when her great-great-grandparents were alive. This also happens to be a time of slavery. Dana is a black woman from the 1970’s who is married to a white man. Each time she is thrown into the past, she has to learn how to live and survive in this time while staying true to herself.  <br/><br/>I love books about time travel. One of my top favorite reads of all time (The Time Travelers Wif</span>
  <span id="freeText7952298507458708192" style="display:none"><i>Kindred</i> is about a woman named Dana who gets transported/time-travels back to the past. She travels way back to the time when her great-great-grandparents were alive. This also happens to be a time of slavery. Dana is a black woman from the 1970’s who is married to a white man. Each time she is thrown into the past, she has to learn how to live and survive in this time while staying true to herself.  <br /><br />I love books about time travel. One of my top favorite reads of all time (The Time Travelers Wife) has it. This was very different from that book, but I still love that aspect of the story. <i>Kindred</i> is such an interesting book. It really made me think and it’s a story that I know will stay with me.   I have never read a book quite like it before. Dana time-travels back whenever her ancestor is in trouble. It can be days later for her, but years have passed in the past. It boggles the mind. <br /><br />It’s heartbreaking and painful to know that things like this really happened in the not so far back past. I loved Dana’s character and how strong and steadfast she was. She stole the show. Rufus is a character you loved to hate. I actually loved seeing him through Dana’s eyes. I wish Kevin’s character would have been more developed but a part of me thinks he was there to show how much things had changed from the past to the present time. <br /><br />  <i>Kindred</i> is part historical fiction and part sci-fi. It’s a complex story that’s thrilling, intense and I couldn’t put it down. I listened to the audio book and thought the narrator did a fantastic job. This is a book I would definitely recommend. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7952298507458708192" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3213365679" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3213365679" />
    <a title="Julie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5211606-julie"><img alt="Julie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505571524p2/5211606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3213365679?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie" href="/user/show/5211606-julie">Julie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5211606-julie?shelf=reading-road-trip-2020">reading-road-trip-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5211606-julie?shelf=nobody-talks-like-this">nobody-talks-like-this</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5211606-julie?shelf=maryland">maryland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3213365679" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2016781321874666667"><b>Reading Road Trip 2020</b><br/><br/>Current location: <b>Maryland</b><br/><br/>Octavia E. Butler's biography could just break your damn heart.<br/><br/>Her father died when she was 10, she had no siblings, her family was poor.<br/><br/>She was a self-described “loner,” a woman who was tall and awkward and friendless. From the recent bits and pieces I researched, as I started this novel, I gathered that her romantic life was either private or nonexistent. (Was she gay? Asexual? Sickly?) As far as I could tell, she had substantial medical issues a</span>
  <span id="freeText2016781321874666667" style="display:none"><b>Reading Road Trip 2020</b><br /><br />Current location: <b>Maryland</b><br /><br />Octavia E. Butler's biography could just break your damn heart.<br /><br />Her father died when she was 10, she had no siblings, her family was poor.<br /><br />She was a self-described “loner,” a woman who was tall and awkward and friendless. From the recent bits and pieces I researched, as I started this novel, I gathered that her romantic life was either private or nonexistent. (Was she gay? Asexual? Sickly?) As far as I could tell, she had substantial medical issues and lived with her mother, and died, far too young, at 58, of a stroke.<br /><br />It's hard not to hum a few bars of Sarah Vaughn's “Lonely Woman” while sorting through Ms. Butler's online photo gallery.<br /><br />And yet. . . she was a writer, and not just any writer, but a <i>female</i> writer of science fiction.<br /><br />This is so extraordinary to me, my closeted sci-fi self has always rejoiced, just knowing that Ms. Butler's work was still out there for me to explore.<br /><br />In fact, while I was researching titles for this figurative road trip of mine, I set aside my devotion to one of my all-time favorite writers, Anne Tyler, to give Ms. Butler's “sci-fi” novel from 1979 a try.<br /><br />(For the record, my favorite novel set in Maryland is Tyler's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77699.Dinner_at_the_Homesick_Restaurant?ac=1&amp;from_search=true&amp;qid=KOlYAAzrHr&amp;rank=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Dinner at the Homesick Restaurant</a> ).<br /><br />So. . . how was <i>Kindred</i>?<br /><br />Conceptually: fantastic! <br /><br />A twenty-something Black woman, married to an older white man in the 1970s, disappears from her new house in California and time travels to 1815 to a plantation near Baltimore, Maryland.<br /><br />The reader isn't given any more info than that. Dana, our time traveling protagonist, appears to be connected to the slave owners in Maryland, and, similar to Henry from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2027454181?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">The Time Traveler's Wife</a>, you are asked merely to suspend your disbelief that this can happen.<br /><br />In other words, if the genre of “science fiction” makes you uncomfortable, take heart, there aren't any other aspects of it here. Just one piece you need to buy into: Dana time travels and, unlike poor Henry from <i>TTTW</i>, she does not arrive naked at her next destination. <br /><br />What Ms. Butler imagined here is juicy and delicious. . . a modern Black woman, married to a white man, is forced to have everything threatened, everything taken away from her. When her husband grabs on to her arm and travels with her on her a later journey, the plot thickens. Wow! What a crazy idea, to throw this couple and their modern ideology into the Southern cookpot. . . So much could happen here!!<br /><br />So. . . does it? <br /><br />Nope.<br /><br />Not only that, the dialogue here is some of the worst I've ever encountered. It wasn't long before this book became the next entry on my “nobody talks like this” shelf.<br /><br /><b>Nobody talks like this</b> to each other, and certainly not two romantic partners. The “verbal” exchanges between our young couple, Dana and Kevin, were flat-out painful for me. <br /><br />I did not experience one page of this read without thinking: this is a book. I'm reading a book.<br /><br />I never, not once, felt as though I had emerged into this world. <br /><br />This novel lacked authentic dialogue, character development, and depth. I felt like I was in a world of cardboard cutouts for characters and poster boards for scenery. <br /><br />On page 208, our time traveling protagonist, Dana, declares, “I wanted so much for it to be over.”<br /><br />So did I, Dana.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2016781321874666667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1891579779" />
    <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters"><img alt="Elyse  Walters" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514246949p2/597461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1891579779?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elyse  Walters" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters">Elyse  Walters</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1891579779" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9015352151844772117">Audiobook/sync/ebook<br/><br/>Voice narrator Kim Staunton was outstanding....<br/>Absolutely fantastic! <br/>At times I felt like I was watching a movie.... and Staunton had a lot to do with the ‘movie-feeling’ experience. She demanded my attention- I even stood taller while soaking in our warm water pool. <br/><br/>The time travel/fantasy/historical fiction blend worked beautifully... kept me interested. <br/>Dana Franklin is a strong protagonist - a black woman married to a white man during the 70’s. <br/>Each time she is thrown bac</span>
  <span id="freeText9015352151844772117" style="display:none">Audiobook/sync/ebook<br /><br />Voice narrator Kim Staunton was outstanding....<br />Absolutely fantastic! <br />At times I felt like I was watching a movie.... and Staunton had a lot to do with the ‘movie-feeling’ experience. She demanded my attention- I even stood taller while soaking in our warm water pool. <br /><br />The time travel/fantasy/historical fiction blend worked beautifully... kept me interested. <br />Dana Franklin is a strong protagonist - a black woman married to a white man during the 70’s. <br />Each time she is thrown back in time she has to grapple with the devastation-slavery-era - vs. remembering who she is: a free woman/born free.<br />Oh my - the children and messages in this book are priceless. <br /><br />The creative crafty storytelling, .....<br />with so much -WRONGNESS - INJUSTICE &amp; DISPARITY had me thinking - as in ‘living’ -in both- the past and present world. <br /><br />This was my first book by Octavia Butler....( sad to know she is no longer alive)...<br />What a beautiful passionate writer she was.<br /><br />I’ve own this book for years ... <br />It was seeing the movie - “Just Mercy” recently- adapted from the incredible book by Bryan Stevenson -<br />And....<br />connecting with author *Hana Ali*, <br />whom I had the privilege of sharing with recently as well....<br />who told me ‘Kim Staunton’...(voice narrator of ‘her’ memoir):<br /> “At Home with Muhammad Ali”. ( a book I’ve not read ‘yet’ but look forward to).....<br />that inspired me to choose “Kindred”....<br />To experience both Octavia &amp; Kim <br /><br />I certainly waited long enough to read this ‘classic’( ?/!), book. <br />and......<br />SO MUCH BETTER than I expected. (as I often tend to shy away from words like sci-fi, fantasy, and time travel)...<br />But honestly&gt;&gt; there was absolutely nothing to worry about - <br />I would’ve missed a wonderful story and introduction to an author I had not read. <br /><br />Blessings &amp; Thanks...to all the readers of this book who came before me.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9015352151844772117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
