<div id="review_791550084" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/791550084" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/791550084?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Sci-fi and history? It's possible</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer791550084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2073287179863064026"><i>Kindred</i> is a hybrid novel, difficult to categorize. Partly science-fiction, partly historical novel, it addresses race, gender and class issues in the context of slavery but, and this is the complexity of this book, in two timelines, antebellum Maryland and modern California. <br/><br/>Butler, far from trying to make sense of time travel and how it suddenly affects the protagonist of the story, uses the sci-fi device to transport a free Afro-American woman to a colonial plantation near Baltimore to explor</span>
  <span id="freeText2073287179863064026" style="display:none"><i>Kindred</i> is a hybrid novel, difficult to categorize. Partly science-fiction, partly historical novel, it addresses race, gender and class issues in the context of slavery but, and this is the complexity of this book, in two timelines, antebellum Maryland and modern California. <br /><br />Butler, far from trying to make sense of time travel and how it suddenly affects the protagonist of the story, uses the sci-fi device to transport a free Afro-American woman to a colonial plantation near Baltimore to explore human resilience when confronted with the vexation, humiliation and manifold forms of abuse, physical and psychological, of treating human beings as property to be used, misused and trafficked with.<br /><br />I wasn’t overly enthusiastic about the time travel episodes but I will confess I was instantly pulled into the story. Dana, the narrator, and maybe even the author’s alter ego, challenges the reader to get to grips with the cruel reality of slaves, of their everyday life and their will to survive mainly with the sole idea of protecting their endangered families from the masters’ whims and vicious “rights”. The artifice of time travel serves the purpose of making historical barbarity a tangible and constant threat and to better understand the huge amount of silent courage required of those oppressed to endure all sorts of inhuman punishments. <br /><br />Butler’s prose is rather unadorned and definitely plot-driven, but she is very accurate in recreating the social mechanisms that enabled slavery in the Southern States; the violence, the helplessness and the loss of whatever self-dignity might be left to those born into captivity. The effects of racial bigotry and subjugation on the making of identity are clearly delineated and open a debate on the reader’s mind. Progress has been made since the days of legal enslavement, of course, but is it enough? <br />Books like this one remind us that we should never lose that silent courage, that will to survive in order to fight injustice and oppression, if only, to pay back the huge sacrifices that our ancestors made so that we could exist, so that we could be here, living a relatively comfortable life, now, today, and hopefully, a life that will be fairer when our children fight their own battles in the future. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2073287179863064026" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1725858460" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1725858460" />
    <a title="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes"><img alt="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598297811p2/1452829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1725858460?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" href="/user/show/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes">Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=owned-physical">owned-physical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=year-2017">year-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1725858460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer415665925625251155"><b>
  <i>"I never realized how easily people could be trained to accept slavery."</i>
</b><br/><br/><b>Actual Rating: 4.5 Stars</b><br/><br/>This was such an <u>excellent</u> book.<br/><br/>Kindred tells the story of Dana Franklin, a black woman who is suddenly whisked back in time from 1976 to pre-Civil War Maryland in 1815. <br/><br/>This novel is a beautifully elegant analysis of a not-so-beautiful period in American history. Using a prominent element of Science Fiction, Butler confronts the poisonous attitudes & double standards that are propagated by racism</span>
  <span id="freeText415665925625251155" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"I never realized how easily people could be trained to accept slavery."</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>Actual Rating: 4.5 Stars</b><br /><br />This was such an <u>excellent</u> book.<br /><br />Kindred tells the story of Dana Franklin, a black woman who is suddenly whisked back in time from 1976 to pre-Civil War Maryland in 1815. <br /><br />This novel is a beautifully elegant analysis of a not-so-beautiful period in American history. Using a prominent element of Science Fiction, Butler confronts the poisonous attitudes &amp; double standards that are propagated by racism, but from a modern perspective. <br /><br />I enjoyed how this book didn't shy away from being <b>brutally honest.</b> It doesn't seek to obscure the cruelty of the time period. It doesn't ask for the approval of its readers. <br /><br />It forces you to <b>examine.</b> <br /><br />We all like to think that we wouldn't have participated in that atrocious behavior that has stained our history. It's easy for us to imagine ourselves as a hero, to pretend we would have loudly &amp; proudly condemned the enslavement of human beings. <br /><br /><b>But would we have?</b><br /><br />Dana's excursions may suggest otherwise. At times she even finds herself adjusting her actions to mold to the past &amp; providing forgiveness where she never would've expected to. <br /><br />To discuss history is to remember it. To remember it is to prevent it from repeating itself. <b>Keeping the conversation alive is vital.</b><br /><br />I would recommend this book to <u>everyone.</u> I only docked half a star because I spotted one or two trivial inconsistencies. Otherwise, a marvelous classic and one I'm sorry I waited this long to pick up.<br /><br /><i><a href="http://booknest.eu/reviews/34-mary-s-reviews/405-kindred" rel="nofollow noopener">This review</a> and other reviews of mine can be found on <a href="http://booknest.eu/" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Nest</a>!</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="415665925625251155" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1843286264" />
    <a title="Joe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61157160-joe"><img alt="Joe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569331508p2/61157160.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1843286264?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/61157160-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1843286264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3810679649256038042">Before Colson Whitehead's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30555488.The_Underground_Railroad' title='The Underground Railroad by Colson Whitehead' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Underground Railroad</a>, there was <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60931.Kindred' title='Kindred by Octavia E. Butler' rel='nofollow noopener'> Kindred</a>, a grueling plunge into American slavery with a fantastic twist. One of the great time travel novels, right there with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40526.Time_and_Again__Time___1_' title='Time and Again (Time, #1) by Jack Finney' rel='nofollow noopener'> Time and Again</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10644930.11_22_63' title='11/22/63 by Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'> 11/22/63</a>. Aspects of the narrative might be too agonizing for the tender at heart, but I was with it all the way, from first sentence to last.</span>
  <span id="freeText3810679649256038042" style="display:none">Before Colson Whitehead's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30555488.The_Underground_Railroad" title="The Underground Railroad by Colson Whitehead" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Underground Railroad</a>, there was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/60931.Kindred" title="Kindred by Octavia E. Butler" rel="nofollow noopener"> Kindred</a>, a grueling plunge into American slavery with a fantastic twist. One of the great time travel novels, right there with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40526.Time_and_Again__Time___1_" title="Time and Again (Time, #1) by Jack Finney" rel="nofollow noopener"> Time and Again</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10644930.11_22_63" title="11/22/63 by Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener"> 11/22/63</a>. Aspects of the narrative might be too agonizing for the tender at heart, but I was with it all the way, from first sentence to last.</span>

<div id="review_277506729" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/277506729" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/277506729?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer277506729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13699971294988371308">BkC10) <i>KINDRED</i> by Octavia E. Butler: Excellent!!<br/><br/>I still agree with myself. And what better review for Valentine's Day than this time-travel novel in which a modern-day African-American woman is summoned by her slave-owning ancestor to rescue him at critical moments, and then must pimp her slave ancestress to the slave owner to ensure that she is born?<br/><br/>Rating: 4.75* of five<br/><br/><b>The Publisher Says</b>: The first science fiction written by a black woman, <i>Kindred</i> has become a cornerstone of black American lit</span>
  <span id="freeText13699971294988371308" style="display:none">BkC10) <i>KINDRED</i> by Octavia E. Butler: Excellent!!<br /><br />I still agree with myself. And what better review for Valentine's Day than this time-travel novel in which a modern-day African-American woman is summoned by her slave-owning ancestor to rescue him at critical moments, and then must pimp her slave ancestress to the slave owner to ensure that she is born?<br /><br />Rating: 4.75* of five<br /><br /><b>The Publisher Says</b>: The first science fiction written by a black woman, <i>Kindred</i> has become a cornerstone of black American literature. This combination of slave memoir, fantasy, and historical fiction is a novel of rich literary complexity. Having just celebrated her 26th birthday in 1976 California, Dana, an African-American woman, is suddenly and inexplicably wrenched through time into antebellum Maryland. After saving a drowning white boy there, she finds herself staring into the barrel of a shotgun and is transported back to the present just in time to save her life. During numerous such time-defying episodes with the same young man, she realizes the challenge she’s been given: to protect this young slaveholder until he can father her own great-grandmother. <br /><br /><b>My Review</b>: Oh my heck. Modern African American, circa 1976, makes her way in the slave society of 1815, and all that that entails. It's not an easy, fluffy read, but it's amazing how Butler pulls no punches and still manages to keep the easy, smug path of good = noble, bad = horrible, from making her characters into masks capable of expressing only one emotion. I liked reading the book because it pulls no punches, and it left me breathless at frequent intervals with its complete willingness to engage all, each, every, facet of human love. <br /><br />Octavia Butler, gone too soon, wrote this meditation on survival when she was abour thirty. What a feat that is. So young, and so sharply critical of denial and misdirection...so ready to face up to the underlying motivations and the foundational lies of each and every character's identity...what she must have been like as a friend! Her insights would be Buddha-like, if they were anything like the honest and unsparing insights she used in creating this book.<br /><br />Beautiful, hard, fiery. Like a diamond, it will cut anything you can show it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13699971294988371308" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_224221581" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/224221581" />
    <a title="Nadine X" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1791308-nadine-x"><img alt="Nadine X" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1398803352p2/1791308.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/224221581?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nadine X" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nadine X" href="/user/show/1791308-nadine-x">Nadine X</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer224221581" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15750752206160243837">I wanted to love this book. But it has many flaws. I'll get to that in a few, but first, let me gush about what's great about it. <br/><br/>The plot/premise is brilliant. I love the idea of a modern black woman being propelled back into time to help one of her white ancestors to survive, even if he becomes a mean and despicable slave master. I love the fact that it used time travel, which I usually hate, but found tolerable here. I love the observations of the protagonist, Dana. She's an interesting chara</span>
  <span id="freeText15750752206160243837" style="display:none">I wanted to love this book. But it has many flaws. I'll get to that in a few, but first, let me gush about what's great about it. <br /><br />The plot/premise is brilliant. I love the idea of a modern black woman being propelled back into time to help one of her white ancestors to survive, even if he becomes a mean and despicable slave master. I love the fact that it used time travel, which I usually hate, but found tolerable here. I love the observations of the protagonist, Dana. She's an interesting character, with a lot of strength, and some flaws that I found believable. <br /><br />The book is easy to read, but perhaps that's where I start my critique. It sometimes over simplifies in an effort to push through the story. There was a lot of opportunity for complexity, and the author sorta takes the easy way out in many instances. Also, it feels a little dumbed down at times, and the author tries too hard to explain some things, and it comes off as contrived. In other instances, though, she doesn't explain enough, like why Dana is traveling through time in the first place. <br /><br />Overall though, it's a really ambitious and brilliant concept, and a fascinating read. We need more African-American novels that use paranormal elements to explore socio-historical issues. I applaud Octavia Butler for taking the risk and pulling it off, mostly. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15750752206160243837" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1890164516" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1890164516" />
    <a title="Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell"><img alt="Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584062476p2/2190064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1890164516?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell" href="/user/show/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell">Nenia ✨️ Socially Awkward Trash Panda ✨️ Campbell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780807083055" href="/book/show/616830.Kindred">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell?shelf=nenia-recommends">nenia-recommends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell?shelf=poc-interracial">poc-interracial</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell?shelf=timelords">timelords</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-socially-awkward-trash-panda-campbell?shelf=just-tear-my-heart-out-why-dont-you">just-tear-my-heart-out-why-dont-you</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1890164516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2651253901776290119"><a href='http://readasaurus.blogspot.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1459070464i/18565492.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><a href='https://www.instagram.com/neniagram/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Instagram</a> || <a href='https://twitter.com/NeniaCampbell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Twitter</a> || <a href='https://www.facebook.com/aficionenias/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Facebook</a> || <a href='http://www.amazon.com/Nenia-Campbell/e/B00AWVRKMS' rel='nofollow noopener'>Amazon</a> || <a href='https://www.pinterest.com/mightbeamisprin/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pinterest</a><br/><br/><br/>There are a lot of books that talk about the antebellum south, especially in romance novels where it is a popular setting, but few seem to capture the sheer unfairness of what it must have been like as a non-white person living in the South in the nineteenth century. I love Octavia Butler's science fiction, but KINDRED is a book that I purposely put off reading because I'd heard it was brutal. Good, but brutal, and utterly unflinching in</span>
  <span id="freeText2651253901776290119" style="display:none"><a href="http://readasaurus.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1459070464i/18565492.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><a href="https://www.instagram.com/neniagram/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> || <a href="https://twitter.com/NeniaCampbell" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> || <a href="https://www.facebook.com/aficionenias/" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> || <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Nenia-Campbell/e/B00AWVRKMS" rel="nofollow noopener">Amazon</a> || <a href="https://www.pinterest.com/mightbeamisprin/" rel="nofollow noopener">Pinterest</a><br><br><br>There are a lot of books that talk about the antebellum south, especially in romance novels where it is a popular setting, but few seem to capture the sheer unfairness of what it must have been like as a non-white person living in the South in the nineteenth century. I love Octavia Butler's science fiction, but KINDRED is a book that I purposely put off reading because I'd heard it was brutal. Good, but brutal, and utterly unflinching in the portrayal of that brutality.<br><br><br><br>Dana is a black woman living in the 1970s. Her husband, Kevin, is white, and both their families disapprove of that union, even in the twentieth century. Things are pretty good for Dana, though; she has a decent job, a husband who loves her, and her own house filled with books. All that changes when one day, without explanation, she's plunged into the past to save a white relative from death.<br><br><br><br>Rufus is the son of a plantation owner, and one of her relatives, the Weylins, although her family history is so occluded that until now, she never realized he was white. There's a bond connecting them, tightening whenever Rufus is about to die - and the only way that Dana is able to return to her own time is when her own life is threatened. Some people have said that this reminded them of OUTLANDER, and that's true: the time-travel is just as sketchy and mysterious, and neither shrink back from cruelty and rape.<br><br><br><br>What makes KINDRED such an interesting book is the complex way that Butler portrays slavery. She makes so much social commentary about both the twentieth century and the nineteenth century, and despite being published about forty years ago, it still feels fresh and modern. Dana struggles with slavery as a modern woman, and yet even she realizes how sinister a trap it is: when you have no rights, any concession feels like a blessing, to the point where you may start to feel affection for someone just treating you like a human being. She experiences something akin to Stockholm syndrome, and sees firsthand how some of her peers struggle and are oppressed by those same societal constraints.<br><br><br><br>KINDRED is not an easy read. There is rape, and torture, and cruelty of all colors. The N-word is bandied around a lot (because this is the South in the nineteenth century, and it would not be realistic otherwise). I think many readers, white readers especially, will probably be shocked at the no-holds barred approach, especially if they're accustomed to the version of history that sugar-coats the antebellum period and has slaves and black servants being adored and treated like family. Dana herself has a similar moment of disillusionment when she is researching the period and picks up a copy of GONE WITH THE WIND, only to put it down in disgust.<br><br><br><br>The fact of the matter is, slavery happened. It happened and it was awful, and it happened. But it's important to know that it happened, and what it was like; it's important to know that real human beings suffered at the hands of other human beings, and were made to feel different based on where they came from and the color of their skin; it's important to know that injustice is a real and painful thing that is mired in our shared history and continues to be perpetuated to this day.<br><br><br><br>It's important to know that, so we remember why we must never go back; and why we must do our best going forward to work towards a future of true equality. We still have a ways to go.<br><br><br><br>4.5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="2651253901776290119" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1737506916" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1737506916" />
    <a title="Book Riot Community" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33447134-book-riot-community"><img alt="Book Riot Community" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583162430p2/33447134.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1737506916?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Book Riot Community" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Book Riot Community" href="/user/show/33447134-book-riot-community">Book Riot Community</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1737506916" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2025702737943769450">Kindred is one of those books that feels like it should be required reading for everyone. It’s a page-turning, disturbing, provocative, complex, incredibly smart novel. Technically it’s science fiction, since it involves time travel, but it doesn’t follow a lot of other SF conventions. Dana, an African-American woman living in late 70s LA, is suddenly taken back to Antebellum Maryland, where she saves a young white boy from drowning. Although she is inexplicably whisked home, she is brought back</span>
  <span id="freeText2025702737943769450" style="display:none">Kindred is one of those books that feels like it should be required reading for everyone. It’s a page-turning, disturbing, provocative, complex, incredibly smart novel. Technically it’s science fiction, since it involves time travel, but it doesn’t follow a lot of other SF conventions. Dana, an African-American woman living in late 70s LA, is suddenly taken back to Antebellum Maryland, where she saves a young white boy from drowning. Although she is inexplicably whisked home, she is brought back to the past again shortly, where she realizes her task is to save the life of this white man who turns out to be her ancestor, over and over and over. If you didn’t already know Octavia Butler was a genius, this book will convince you.<br /><br />— Casey Stepaniuk<br /><br />from The Best Books We Read In September 2016: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2016/10/03/riot-round-up-the-best-books-we-read-in-september-2/">http://bookriot.com/2016/10/03/riot-r...</a><br /><br />____________________<br /><br /><br />I admit that I am usually too hesitant to pick up books about slavery. This year I’m trying to get over my hesitation and this was one of my biggest overlooked books. Even though it’s a few decades old, Kindred really holds up. Every time I thought it might be getting a little problematic, it was just me not seeing that Butler was setting me up to address the very issue that was making me uncomfortable. She stares harsh truths and prejudices right in the face and she also creates a story you cannot stop reading.<br /><br />–Jessica Woodbury<br /><br /><br /><br />from The Best Books We Read In July 2016: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://bookriot.com/2016/08/01/riot-round-up-the-best-books-we-read-in-july-4/">http://bookriot.com/2016/08/01/riot-r...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2025702737943769450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2278076252" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2278076252" />
    <a title="Liz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4125660-liz"><img alt="Liz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316269997p2/4125660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2278076252?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz" href="/user/show/4125660-liz">Liz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18889555-kindred">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4125660-liz?shelf=best-of-2018">best-of-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2278076252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14172691971588085365"><br/>In honor of Science Fiction and Fantasy Week, I am finally taking this book from my TBR queue and actually reading it. The author called this a “grim fantasy” and the description fits. I’m not a big fan of SF or fantasy, but a dear friend of mine has been touting this book for ages and it’s time for me to set aside the netgalley queue for a few days. <br/><br/>Dana becomes the victim of unwanted time travel. She doesn’t know why it’s happening but all of a sudden she’s being transported back to early 1800</span>
  <span id="freeText14172691971588085365" style="display:none"><br />In honor of Science Fiction and Fantasy Week, I am finally taking this book from my TBR queue and actually reading it. The author called this a “grim fantasy” and the description fits. I’m not a big fan of SF or fantasy, but a dear friend of mine has been touting this book for ages and it’s time for me to set aside the netgalley queue for a few days. <br /><br />Dana becomes the victim of unwanted time travel. She doesn’t know why it’s happening but all of a sudden she’s being transported back to early 1800s Maryland Eastern Shore and her ancestors. The book doesn’t bother dealing with why or how happens. The book’s strength lies in her ability to portray the time, place and personalities of the time. The other strength is comparing how Kevin and Dana see what’s happening around them, male vs. female, white vs. black. This part makes the book especially timely, given the current conversations in our society. <br /><br />I don’t normally care for books involving time travel or other non realistic ploys. But it works well to be able to compare modern day sensibilities and past beliefs. It also works well to show how quickly one can come to accept what you’re surrounded by, even when those surroundings would have formerly offended your sensibilities. “The ease. Us, the children...I never realized how easily people could be trained to accept slavery.”<br /><br />The book is totally engrossing. I read this quickly, incapable of staying away from it. Fair warning, parts of this book are exceedingly dark and not for the faint hearted. But it’s an amazing story. Highly recommend. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14172691971588085365" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1387737468" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1387737468" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1387737468?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=traditional-fiction">traditional-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1387737468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11593532824238942032">This is pretty much a historical novel with a bit of SF icing, focusing almost exclusively on the relationships built between a mid-1970's modern black woman who is continually sent back in time to save an ancestor from an early death. Unfortunately for her, she's a black woman on a slave plantation, and she's stuck there for a disproportionately long time, sometimes even bringing her white husband back into the past with her and sometimes leaving him behind. Theres a ton of time dilation, where</span>
  <span id="freeText11593532824238942032" style="display:none">This is pretty much a historical novel with a bit of SF icing, focusing almost exclusively on the relationships built between a mid-1970's modern black woman who is continually sent back in time to save an ancestor from an early death. Unfortunately for her, she's a black woman on a slave plantation, and she's stuck there for a disproportionately long time, sometimes even bringing her white husband back into the past with her and sometimes leaving him behind. Theres a ton of time dilation, where moments pass in the modern world and years pass in the past, so it brings a real sense of horror to the story as her real life tumbles away into long absences with her husband.<br /><br />Of course, the real story isn't about Dana and Kevin, our moderns. Or at least, their intro brings to life a closer contrast of living as a slave, and not always entirely that different from those living in such humiliating circumstances on the plantation. Sure, science, medicine, the abolition of slavery, the freedom to speak your mind, all of that is very well and good, but it says a lot right out of the gate that Dana was able to fit into the frankly horrifying life of the past without too much struggle. Life as a temp slave as she struggled with her dream to become a writer seemed to be merely an appetizer before the grand meal of humiliation and torture.<br /><br />I can make a pretty solid argument that most people live an all too-similar picture of daily grind and humiliation, and it's only in the matter of degree that anything has really changed, either that, or it's a matter of sublimation.<br /><br />Dana was seen by too many of those past slaves as an black who pretended to be white because she was educated and tried, with varied success, to stand up to the one person she was nearly irrevocably tied to: Rufus, the son of the plantation owner, who despite Dana's best efforts, still turned out to be a fucking ass. Is it merely cultural? Is it thoroughly cultural? Butler's argument really seems to push aside individual will, time and time again, with every push in the right direction met with an equally feverish backlash.<br /><br />Sure, we could have had these reversals take place as a science fictional trope, but Butler does something much more interesting. She blames people for being people.<br /><br />Those times were a travesty of human stupidity and misery, and it's an even bigger blow to us as a species that it's hardly isolated or unique. We live in our own version of slavery, still, even if so many of the particulars have improved, we're still weighted by expectations, assumptions, and bloody-mindedness no less destructive.<br /><br />Be kinder to our writers. Give them an outlet to create wonderful mirrors to ourselves, such as this novel.<br /><br />I'm very sorry to know that Butler had passed away, and I'm sorry that it has taken me these many years to finally come around to reading her for the first time. If she was still around, I'd like to thank her. Since she isn't, I'll thank everyone who still remembers this book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11593532824238942032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1833171396" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1833171396" />
    <a title="Taryn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1334938-taryn"><img alt="Taryn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407768387p2/1334938.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1833171396?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Taryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Taryn" href="/user/show/1334938-taryn">Taryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1334938-taryn?shelf=transcends-the-star-scale">transcends-the-star-scale</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1833171396" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7903572586558030956">Without any warning, Dana Franklin is thrust back through time and space. It's 1976 and she's settling into her new California apartment when she starts to feel dizzy. Her modern surroundings fade away and suddenly she's in antebellum Maryland. She seems to be inextricably linked with Rufus Weylin, the young son of a plantation owner. Dana is pulled to Rufus anytime his life is in danger, which happens with surprising frequency. The era is dangerous for Dana--she's black and has no enforceable r</span>
  <span id="freeText7903572586558030956" style="display:none">Without any warning, Dana Franklin is thrust back through time and space. It's 1976 and she's settling into her new California apartment when she starts to feel dizzy. Her modern surroundings fade away and suddenly she's in antebellum Maryland. She seems to be inextricably linked with Rufus Weylin, the young son of a plantation owner. Dana is pulled to Rufus anytime his life is in danger, which happens with surprising frequency. The era is dangerous for Dana--she's black and has no enforceable rights. It turns out that she and Rufus both need each other to survive, but they are also capable of destroying each other.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>Slavery was a long slow process of dulling.</em>
</blockquote><br /><br /><strong><em>Kindred</em> is a quick read because the language is plain and it's dialogue-heavy</strong>, but there's so much to unpack. I was surprised that the logistics of Dana's time traveling were never addressed, but the time traveling is just a framework to explore the themes. <strong>Octavia Butler describes it as a "grim fantasy" and there's no science.</strong> Dana's story begins in 1976, two hundred years after the adoption of the Declaration of Independence. <strong>It's such a whirlwind!</strong> <strong>Because of the mechanics of Dana's time traveling, each chapter begins and ends with a life-threatening situation.</strong> The chapters have titles like <em>The Fire</em>, <em>The Fall</em>, etc. that give hints of what's to come and add to the intensity. Rufus is a few years older every time Dana returns to the plantation, so we get to watch him and the other characters as they grow up and their roles on the plantation evolve. Dana is always marked by her experience when she's transported back to California, sometimes permanently. When we first meet Dana, she is laying in a modern hospital bed after her left arm has been amputated.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>“The ease seemed so frightening,” I said. “Now I see why.” “What?” “The ease. Us, the children … I never realized how easily people could be trained to accept slavery.”</em>
</blockquote><br /><br /><strong>Dana was intellectually aware of the horrors of slavery, but her education doesn't prepare her for the brutality she actually experiences.</strong> She sees how inadequate Hollywood is at portraying the real thing. <strong>Dana is shocked at the ease with which people settle into their institutionally-defined roles, herself included.</strong> She's especially shaken when she sees the children playing slave-trading games. Dana notices that despite the disdain with which we view slavery now, wide-scale, government-sanctioned oppression has been allowed to occur repeatedly in the modern era. She specifically mentions Nazi Germany and apartheid in South Africa.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>“Better to stay alive," I said. "At least while there's a chance to get free." I thought of the sleeping pills in my bag and wondered just how great a hypocrite I was. It was so easy to advise other people to live with their pain.</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />Like many slave-owners, the Weylins use family attachments to keep the slaves in line. Dana's hands are similarly tied by family; her entire ancestral lineage depends on Rufus. <strong>Dana has to betray her modern values in order to ensure her eventual birth.</strong> In an era with only bad and worse choices, she begins to empathize more with the slaves on the plantation. With hindsight, it's easy to create caricatures of those in the past and forget that they are complex human beings with nuanced relationships.<strong> Dana sees firsthand how privileged it is to judge someone's actions from a safe distance and how sometimes what seems like the best course of action can have terrible ramifications.</strong> But even Dana falls into the trap of feeling superior to others: <em>"I looked down on her myself for a while. Moral superiority. Here was someone even less courageous than I was. That comforted me somehow." </em><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>His father wasn’t the monster he could have been with the power he held over his slaves. He wasn’t a monster at all. Just an ordinary man who sometimes did the monstrous things his society said were legal and proper.</em>
</blockquote><br /><br />In Han Kang's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28172483-human-acts?from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">Human Acts</a>, there's a section about how everyone has the potential for good and evil and a character wonders how humans can be directed towards the more humane path. <em>Kindred</em> explores how institutions drive behaviors and make it hard to change anything. Sometimes there were glimpses of potential goodness in the Weylin men, but the brutality always wins out. <strong>Dana tries to exert a progressive influence on Rufus, but it's an impossible task when everything else in his world is working against her efforts.</strong> <br /><br />There are some parallels between Dana's life in the 1800s and the 1970s. In 1976, Dana works what at she calls <em>"the slave market,"</em> a temp agency where <em>"nonpeople rented for a few hours a few days, a few weeks."</em> At first, it made me think about the casualness with which words like "slave" and "Nazi" are thrown about in the modern world. But as the parallels continued, I also began to see the way<strong> the remnants of the past worm their way into our present and the basic human impulses that can snowball into something horrific. </strong><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>Strangely, they seemed to like [Rufus], hold him in contempt, and fear him all at the same time. This confused me because I felt just about the same mixture of emotions for him myself. I had thought my feelings were complicated because he and I had such a strange relationship. But then, slavery of any kind fostered strange relationships. Only the overseer drew simple, unconflicting emotions of hatred and fear when he appeared briefly. But then, it was part of the overseer’s job to be hated and feared while the master kept his hands clean. </em>
</blockquote><br /><br />At one point <strong>Dana's husband Kevin, a white man,</strong> is also transported to the Weylin plantation, which adds some additional complications. His presence makes it safer for Dana because he can pretend to be her owner, but what happens if she gets transported back to 1976 and he's unable to grab onto her in time? She's worried about what this era will do to him. Will it destroy him or rub off on him? <strong>Kevin is essentially a good man, but sometimes clueless.</strong> I expected more from him, so sometimes I'd be as angry with him as I was with Rufus. Like Rufus, he is more progressive than his forebearers but he's still a man of his time. <strong>There are times in both 1976 and the 1800s where he's unable to see outside the lens of his own experience.</strong> He remarks on how plantation life isn't as bad as he would've expected, unaware of how different his experience is from Dana's experience and <strong>unable to confront the true horror of something he'll never be subjected to. </strong><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>Repressive societies always seemed to understand the danger of “wrong” ideas.</em>
</blockquote><br /><br /><strong>The most jarring thing about Dana's character is she quickly accepts her fantastic situation. She is somewhat detached from her experiences.</strong> Much of that is purposeful as she eases into her role, but maintains an observer's distance. However, she remains remarkably levelheaded from the beginning. While Dana sometimes felt "empty" to me, Butler really brings the supporting cast to life: Alice, Luke, Nigel, Carrie, Sarah, and even characters with smaller roles like Sam. Maybe Dana was written the way she was so that the reader could essentially "inhabit" her body and become a time traveler themselves.<br /><br />My edition included a critical essay by Robert Crossley, which includes information on Octavia Butler's background as well as analysis of <i>Kindred</i>. There were some aspects I picked up on, but many I didn't. Because of my prior knowledge and the book cover, I didn't notice that Dana's race was withheld until a specific moment. I loved learning about Butler's mischievous reasoning behind choosing a spouse for Dana. Crossley also writes about how the science fiction landscape and the nature of the "alien" changed with the inclusion of female authors, which was really interesting.<br /><br />I've been meaning to read <em>Kindred</em> for a long time, but I just now made time for it in preparation to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28818221-kindred" rel="nofollow noopener">Kindred: A Graphic Novel Adaptation</a> (review coming soon). <strong>One thing is clear: I need more Octavia Butler in my life!</strong> I think those that enjoy this book might want to try <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30555488-the-underground-railroad?ac=1&amp;from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">The Underground Railroad</a> by Colson Whitehead, which addresses slavery but plays with time in a different way. Even if you didn't like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30555488-the-underground-railroad?ac=1&amp;from_search=true" rel="nofollow noopener">The Underground Railroad</a> because some of the liberties the author you took, you might want to give <em>Kindred</em> a try. Despite the time travel, it keeps an accurate historical timeline.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7903572586558030956" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1263956268" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1263956268" />
    <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton"><img alt="J.L.   Sutton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438142892p2/3675162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1263956268?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.L.   Sutton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.L.   Sutton" href="/user/show/3675162-j-l-sutton">J.L.   Sutton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1263956268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16296716128069505701">I enjoyed Octavia Butler's Kindred, but it might disappoint those who are expecting what is billed as science fiction. Despite writing a novel based on time travel, Butler never addresses the mechanics of this time travel or whether perhaps there is something about the protagonist, Dana Franklin, which triggers it. Instead, Butler relies on establishing a bond between Dana and the boy she rescues the first time she is thrust into the past. This does not detract at all from the emotional impact o</span>
  <span id="freeText16296716128069505701" style="display:none">I enjoyed Octavia Butler's Kindred, but it might disappoint those who are expecting what is billed as science fiction. Despite writing a novel based on time travel, Butler never addresses the mechanics of this time travel or whether perhaps there is something about the protagonist, Dana Franklin, which triggers it. Instead, Butler relies on establishing a bond between Dana and the boy she rescues the first time she is thrust into the past. This does not detract at all from the emotional impact of the novel; Butler understands how to tell a compelling story. Subsequent trips back into the past reveal the bond between the two and why Dana needs to save and protect him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16296716128069505701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2581479912" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2581479912" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51395417-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506113266p2/51395417.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2581479912?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/51395417-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2581479912" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1011260801404763214">“She had done the safe thing-had accepted a life of slavery because she was afraid.”<br/><br/>“Kindred” is a novel I would not have picked up on my own, but it was a book club selection so I dutifully read it. Although I do not think it is a great text, it is a good story. When I accepted the limitations inherent in a story about time travel, and focused on the aspects of the writing that are quite good, and not those that are weak, I found I read it quickly and was no worse for the wear.<br/>In short, the sto</span>
  <span id="freeText1011260801404763214" style="display:none">“She had done the safe thing-had accepted a life of slavery because she was afraid.”<br /><br />“Kindred” is a novel I would not have picked up on my own, but it was a book club selection so I dutifully read it. Although I do not think it is a great text, it is a good story. When I accepted the limitations inherent in a story about time travel, and focused on the aspects of the writing that are quite good, and not those that are weak, I found I read it quickly and was no worse for the wear.<br />In short, the story is that of a black woman from 1976 who is transported back and forth to 1815 and the Maryland farm on which her great great grandfather (a white man) and her great great grandmother (a slave) meet. Octavia Butler (the author) does not flinch from the brutality in slavery, and yet it is evident (from an author interview in the text and from the book itself) that she is using the daily aspects of slavery in service to her story, which thus gives them more power. As she says, “The route to readers’ heads is through their guts and nerves, and that requires good storytelling, not just a good set of issues.” With that in mind, she wrote a novel that is not polemic, a danger for any book with a focus like this one.<br />As I read "Kindred” I kept thinking this is a text that would be good for high school students. The characterization is not particularly deep for any one character (is that a limitation of the science fiction genre?) yet there is enough there to discuss and extrapolate a bit more from. At times I felt the book took the easy way out by not examining some issue further, but again that is not the kind of book Ms. Butler was trying to write. This is not “Beloved” and never was meant to be. Yet, serious issues are there if you care to focus on them, and they are given thoughtful attention. There are numerous times in the novel where some idea or thought was beautifully rendered and I had to stop to appreciate and digest it. However, you could also gloss over them if you so choose and just read a historical time travel science fiction novel. <br />The choice is the readers, and I think either is probably correct.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1011260801404763214" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('vWy4IYLpsS90xtUlu5mmRW+PDLvemZcXg533yeDWBAhQMIrWxjGKCPPsMpXTZu0lD1ZdbZBy7Y30VfHefafOlQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yZbzmmzsz4notJREakkPLa18liYd9iz8fDzxX5sneSUkysFtKDT0rm+ec/QCtkRNzaXH8FMdVmYL9PdIBlazuA==')})
  }

Effia is married off to an Englishman and lives in comfort in the palatial rooms of Cape Coast Castle. One thread of Homegoing follows Effia\'s descendants through centuries of warfare in Ghana, as the Fante and Asante nations wrestle with the slave trade and British colonization. The other thread follows Esi and her children into America. From the plantations of the South to the Civil War and the Great Migration, from the coal mines of Pratt City, Alabama, to the jazz clubs and dope houses of twentieth-century Harlem, right up through the present day, Homegoing makes history visceral, and captures, with singular and stunning immediacy, how the memory of captivity came to be inscribed in the soul of a nation. \n\nGeneration after generation, Yaa Gyasi\'s magisterial first novel sets the fate of the individual against the obliterating movements of time, delivering unforgettable characters whose lives were shaped by historical forces beyond their control.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/29535.Octavia_E_Butler">About Octavia E. Butler</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/29535.Octavia_E_Butler"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1242244143p3/29535.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/29535.Octavia_E_Butler">Octavia E. Butler
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
9,572
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer260979282910007799">Octavia Estelle Butler was an American science fiction writer, one of the best-known among the few African-American women in the field. She won both Hugo and Nebula awards. In 1995, she became the first science fiction writer to receive the MacArthur Foundation "Genius" Grant.</span>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
